How gracefully, in correspondence with his words, his free arm or hand — sometimes his head or even his lithe form — moves in quiet gesture, while the grave, receptive apothecary takes into his meditative mind, as into a large, cool cistern, the valued rain-fall of his friend's communications. They are near enough for the little doctor easily to call them; but he is silent. The unhappy feel so far away from the happy. Yet — "Take care!" comes suddenly to his lips, and is almost spoken; for the two, about to cross toward the Place d'Armes at the very spot where Aurora had once made her narrow escape, draw suddenly back, while the black driver of a volante reins up the horse he bestrides, and the animal himself swerves and stops.

The two friends, though startled apart, hasten with lifted hats to the side of the volante, profoundly convinced that one at least, of its two occupants, is heartily sorry that they were not rolled in the dust. Ah, ah! with what a wicked, ill-stifled merriment those two ethereal women bent forward in the faintly perfumed clouds of their ravishing summer-evening garb, to express their equivocal mortification and regret.

"Oh! I'm so sawry, oh! Almoze runned o — ah, ha, ha, ha!"

Aurora could keep the laugh back no longer.

"An' righd yeh befo' haivry boddie! Ah, ha, ha! 'Sieur Grandissime, 'tis me-e-e w'ad know 'ow dad is bad, ha, ha, ha! Oh! I assu' you, gen'lemen, id is hawful!"

And so on.

By and by Honoré seemed urging them to do something, the thought of which made them laugh, yet was entertained as not entirely absurd. It may have been that to which they presently seemed to consent; they alighted from the volante, dismissed it, and walked each at a partner's side down the grassy avenue of the levee. It was as Clotilde with one hand swept her light robes into perfect adjustment for the walk, and turned to take the first step with Frowenfeld, that she raised her eyes for the merest instant to his, and there passed between them an exchange of glance which made the heart of the little doctor suddenly burn like a ball of fire.

"Now we're all right," he murmured bitterly to himself, as, without having seen him, she took the arm of the apothecary, and they moved away.

Yes, if his irony was meant for this pair, he divined correctly. Their hearts had found utterance across the lips, and the future stood waiting for them on the threshold of a new existence, to usher them into a perpetual copartnership in all its joys and sorrows, its disappointments, its imperishable hopes, its aims, its conflicts, its rewards; and the true — the great — the everlasting God of love was with them. Yes, it had been "all right," now, for nearly twenty-four hours — an age of bliss. And now, as they walked beneath the willows where so many lovers had walked before them, they had whole histories to tell of the tremors, the dismays, the misconstructions and longings through which their hearts had come to this bliss; how at such a time, thus and so; and after such and such a meeting, so and so; no part of which was heard by alien ears, except a fragment of Clotilde's speech caught by a small boy in unintentioned ambush.

" — Evva sinze de firze nighd w'en I big-in to nurse you wid de fivver."

She was telling him, with that new, sweet boldness so wonderful to a lately accepted lover, how long she had loved him.

Later on they parted at the porte-cochère. Honoré and Aurora had got there before them, and were passing on up the stairs. Clotilde, catching, a moment before, a glimpse of her face, had seen that there was something wrong; weather-wise as to its indications she perceived an impending shower of tears. A faint shade of anxiety rested an instant on her own face. Frowenfeld could not go in. They paused a little within the obscurity of the corridor, and just to reassure themselves that everything was "all right," they —

God be praised for love's young dream!

"They paused a little within the obscurity of the corridor, and just to reassure themselves that everything was 'all right,'" . . .

The slippered feet of the happy girl, as she slowly mounted the stair alone, overburdened with the weight of her blissful reverie, made no sound. As she turned its mid-angle she remembered Aurora. She could guess pretty well the source of her trouble; Honoré was trying to treat that hand-clasping at the bedside of Agricola as a binding compact; "which, of course, was not fair." She supposed they would have gone into the front drawing-room; she would go into the back. But she miscalculated; as she silently entered the door she saw Aurora standing a little way beyond her, close before Honoré, her eyes cast down, and the trembling fan hanging from her two hands like a broken pinion. He seemed to be reiterating, in a tender undertone, some question intended to bring her to a decision. She lifted up her eyes toward his with a mute, frightened glance.

The intruder, with an involuntary murmur of apology, drew back; but, as she turned, she was suddenly and unspeakably saddened to see Aurora drop her glance, and, with a solemn slowness whose momentous significance was not to be mistaken, silently shake her head.

"Alas!" cried the tender heart of Clotilde. "Alas! M. Grandissime!"

CHAPTER LXI.
"NO!"

IF M. Grandissime had believed that he was prepared for the supreme bitterness of that moment, he had sadly erred. He could not speak. He extended his hand in a dumb farewell, when, all unsanctioned by his will, the voice of despair escaped him in a low groan. At the same moment, a tinkling sound drew near, and the room, which had grown dark with the fall of night, began to brighten with the softly widening light of an evening lamp, as a servant approached to place it in the front drawing-room.

Aurora gave her hand and withdrew it. In the act the two somewhat changed position, and the rays of the lamp, as the maid passed the door, falling upon Aurora's face, betrayed the again upturned eyes.

" 'Sieur Grandissime — "

They fell.

The lover paused.

"You thing I'm crool."

She was the statue of meekness.

"Hope has been cruel to me," replied M. Grandissime, "not you; that I cannot say. Adieu."

He was turning.

" 'Sieur Grandissime — "

She seemed to tremble.

He stood still.

" 'Sieur Grandissime," — her voice was very tender, — "wad you' horry?"

There was a great silence.

" 'Sieur Grandissime, you know — teg a chair."

He hesitated a moment and then both sat down. The servant repassed the door; yet when Aurora broke the silence, she spoke in English — having such hazardous things to say. It would conceal possible stammerings.

" 'Sieur Grandissime — you know dad riz'n I — "

She slightly opened her fan, looking down upon it, and was still.

"I have no right to ask the reason," said M. Grandissime. "It is yours — not mine."

Her head went lower.

"Well, you know," — she drooped it meditatively to one side, with her eyes on the floor, — " 'tis bick-ause — 'tis bick-ause I thing in a few days I'm goin' to die."

M. Grandissime said never a word. He was not alarmed.

She looked up suddenly and took a quick breath, as if to resume, but her eyes fell before his, and she said, in a tone of half-soliloquy:

"I 'ave so mudge troub' wit dad hawt."

She lifted one little hand feebly to the cardiac region, and sighed softly, with a dying languor.

M. Grandissime gave no response. A vehicle rumbled by in the street below, and passed away. At the bottom of the room, where a gilded Mars was driving into battle, a soft note told the half-hour. The lady spoke again.

"Id mague" — she sighed once more — "so strange, — sometime' I thing I'm git'n' crezzy."

Still he to whom these fearful disclosures were being made remained as silent and motionless as an Indian captive, and, after another pause, with its painful accompaniment of small sounds, the fair speaker resumed with more energy, as befitting the approach to an incredible climax:

"Some day', 'Sieur Grandissime, — id mague me fo'gid my hage! I thing I'm young!"

She lifted her eyes with the evident determination to meet his own squarely, but it was too much; they fell as before; yet she went on speaking:

"An' w'en someboddie git'n' ti'ed livin' wid 'imsev an' big'n' to fill ole, an' wan' someboddie to teg de care of 'im an' wan' me to gid marri'd wid 'im — I thin' 'e's in love to me." Her fingers kept up a little shuffling with the fan. "I thing I'm crezzy. I thing I muz be go'n' to die torecklie." She looked up to the ceiling with large eyes, and then again at the fan in her lap, which continued its spreading and shutting. "An' daz de riz'n, 'Sieur Grandissime." She waited until it was certain he was about to answer, and then interrupted him nervously: "You know 'Sieur Grandissime, id woon be righd! Id woon be de juztiz to you! An' you de bez man I evva know in my life,'Sieur Grandissime!" Her hands shook. "A man w'at nevva wan' to gid marri'd wid noboddie in 'is life, and now trine to gid marri'd juz only to rip-ose de soul of 'is oncl' — "

M. Grandissime uttered an exclamation of protest, and she ceased.

"I asked you," continued he, with low-toned emphasis, "for the single and only reason that I want you for my wife."

"Yez," she quickly replied; "daz all. Daz wad I thing. An' I thing daz de rad weh to say, 'Sieur Grandissime. Bick-ause, you know, you an' me is too hole to talg aboud dad lovin', you know. An' you godd dad grade rizpeg fo' me, an' me I godd dad 'ighez rispeg fo' you; bud — " she clutched the fan and her face sank lower still — "bud — " she swallowed — shook her head — "bud — " She bit her lip; she could not go on.

"Aurora," said her lover, bending forward and taking one of her hands. "I do love you with all my soul."

She made a poor attempt to withdraw her hand, abandoned the effort, and looked up savagely through a pair of overflowing eyes, demanding:

"Mais, fo' w'y you di'n' wan' to sesso?"

M. Grandissime smiled argumentatively.

"I have said so a hundred times, in every way but in words."

She lifted her head proudly, and bowed like a queen.

"Mais, you see, 'Sieur Grandissime, you bin meg one mizteg."

"Bud 'tis corrected in time," exclaimed he, with suppressed but eager joyousness.

" 'Sieur Grandissime," she said with a tremendous solemnity, "I'm verrie sawrie, mais — you spogue too lade."

"No, no!" he cried, "the correction comes in time. Say that, lady; say that!"

His ardent gaze beat hers once more down; but she shook her head. He ignored the motion.

"And you will correct your answer; ah! say that, too!" he insisted, covering the captive hand with both his own, and leaning forward from his seat.

"Mais, 'Sieur Grandissime, you know, dad is so verrie unegspeg'."

"Oh! unexpected!"

"Mais, I was thing all dad time id was Clotilde wad you — "

She turned her face away and buried her mouth in her handkerchief.

"Ah!" he cried, "mock me no more, Aurore Nancanou!"

He rose erect and held the hand firmly which she strove to draw away:

"Say the word, sweet lady; say the word!"

She turned upon him suddenly, rose to her feet, was speechless an instant while her eyes flashed into his, and crying out:

"No!" burst into tears, laughed through them, and let him clasp her to his bosom.

Notes

  1. Masked Batteries. Cable is applying a military term to the masked ball described in Chapter I. 'Masked battery' referred to a group of artillery that were hidden from the enemy until they opened fire. Cable is hinting that the events of the masked ball will lead to conflict.
  2. 1803. The last year of European (French) control. In December of the same year, Louisiana became an American territory.
  3. Creole. A person born in the Colonies.
  4. Terpsichore. Greek Τερψιχóρη. In New Orleans, commonly pronounced TERP-si-core. One of the nine Muses; this goddess was was the Muse of dance and chorus of drama. (Her name contains the word for chorus.) The Greek chorus sang and danced in costumes and masks, making Terpsichore a fitting Muse for a masquerade ball.
  5. Bal masqué. A masquerade ball.
  6. Théatre St. Philippe. Actually, it was the second oldest theater. It was built in 1807 on St. Phillip St. between Royal and Bourbon streets, where it operated until 1832.
  7. Brahmin Mandarin Fusilier de Grandissime. These names all have symbolic meanings:
    Brahmin. A member of the upper caste in traditional India.
    Mandarin. A high-ranking official in Imperial China; more generally, someone who commands respect.
    Fusilier. A French soldier armed with a flintlock musket called the the fusil.
    Grandissime. Grandest.
  8. "Comment to yé, Citoyen Agricola!". "How are you, Citizen Agricola!" Citoyen was used as the equivalent of 'Mr.' in Revolutionary France.
  9. Tutoiement. Using the informal tu in speaking to someone.
  10. "Don't you know your ancestors, my little son!" The Queen translates her own French here.
  11. Queen of the Tchoupitoulas. The Tchoupitoulas were a now-extinct tribe of Native Americans. The Fusiliers of the story are descended from their Queen and and Epaminondas Fusilier.
  12. Epaminondas. Epaminondas of Thebes (418 BC - 362 BC) led the Thebans to break the power of Sparta, first by defeating them militarily and then by freeing the Helots, whom the Spartans had enslaved for over two centuries. Because the Helots did all the labor in Sparta, the Spartan citizens had been able to devote their lives to military pursuits.
  13. Dragoons. Light cavalry.
  14. Bienville. Jean-Baptise Le Moyne, Sieur de Bienville (February 23, 1680 – March 7, 1767) was an early governor of Louisiana and the founder of New Orleans.
  15. Filles à la Cassette. "Casket girls" were young, marriageable women sent to New Orleans by France in 1728 to supply the male colonists with wives. At the time, casket meant a small box rather than a coffin. Here is refers to the small chests the women kept their belongings in.
  16. Iberville. Pierre Le Moyne d'Iberville (16 July 1661 – 9 July 1702) was the older brother of Bienville and also an early governor of Louisiana.
  17. Huguenotte. French Protestants who were influenced by the Calvinist theology of Geneva, Switzerland. Their theology was close to that of the Puritans who settled in New England.
  18. Chérie, chérie. Dear, dear.
  19. Bredouille. Empty-handed.
  20. The enemies of religion. The Catholics view of the Huguenottes.
  21. Frowenfeld. Frowenfeld, like Cable, is a German-American; Joseph Frowenfeld stands in contrast to the local population as an English-speaking Protestant with north European origins. He was part of a wave of Americans coming to Louisiana after the Louisiana Purchase.
  22. Evenings and mornings were the first days of the half-built world. A reference to Genesis 1 and the creation of the world. To the new arrival, Louisiana seems unfinished.
  23. Cabildo. The Cabildo had been the seat of the Spanish government. It is now part of the Louisiana State Museum.
  24. Calabozo. Calaboose, jail.
  25. Fever. Yellow fever, which was spread by mosquitoes. Even before people knew that mosquitoes spread the disease, they knew that people who were outside around twilight were more likely to get yellow fever.
  26. Madras handkerchief. A brightly-colored cotton cloth sometimes worn as a turban.
  27. The room shut tight. Because they thought the air itself was tainted with fever.
  28. "Li pa' oulé vini 'ci — li pas capabe." "She wants to come, but she isn't able." [Editors' best guess]
  29. Back-set. Setback, relapse.
  30. Great solitude. Magna civitas, magna solitudo: A great city is a great solitude.
  31. Morphy. Paul Charles Morphy (June 22, 1837 - July 10, 1884) was a New Orleanian who was the greatest chess player in the world during his time.
  32. Quadroon ball. 'Quadroon' was a racial term technically referring to person ¾ white and ¼ black. Quadroon balls were dances held between mixed race women and white men.
  33. "Flower in the crannied wall." Tennyson.
  34. Calumet. Pipe.
  35. Smite the fairest and most distant descendant of an African on the face. Agricola and Honoré both had mixed ancestry, but because Agricola was descended from a Native American, he considered himself white. Cable is here pointing out the fundamental hypocrisy and nonsense of the racial caste structure.
  36. Manitou. A supernatural energy which, in Algonquian lore, permeates nature.
  37. Sagamite. A native-American stew made from corn flour an other ingredients. It was served at ceremonial occasions.
  38. Lettre de cachet. A letter sealed with wax and the royal seal (cachet) of the king.
  39. Pierre Rigaut, the magnificent, the "Grand Marquis," the Governor, De Vaudreuil. Pierre de Rigaud de Vaudreuil de Cavagnal, Marquis de Vaudreuil (November 22, 1698 - August 4, 1778) was the governor of Louisiana 1743-1753.
  40. Louis Quinze. King Louis XV.
  41. House-of-Correction-girl. France rounded up women from the correction houses and sent them to the colonies, much as England sent convicts to the colony at Botany Bay.
  42. She would neither marry nor pray to Mary. Clotilde would not marry any of the French officers, but she would not become a nun either because she was a Protestant Huguenot.
  43. Corsair. A pirate.
  44. Culverin. A small cannon firing 18 pound shells and used commonly in the 17th and 18th centuries.
  45. Olympian rogue of a boy. Cupid.
  46. Cannes Brulées. "Burnt canes." Located in what is now Kenner, LA, a suburb of New Orleans.
  47. Galvez. Bernardo de Gálvez y Madrid, Viscount of Galveston and Count of Gálvez (July 23, 1746 - November 30, 1786), Spanish governor of Louisiana 1777-1785. He was an effective military commander and helped the Americans defeat England during the Revolutionary War.
  48. Hebe. Greek goddess of youth.
  49. Fausse Rivière. False River near Point Coupée and New Roads, LA. On the other side of Baton Rouge, it was rather remote from the activities of New Orleans.
  50. Perfectly pure blood. This novel is largely a critique of the Southern obsession with "purity." Purity involved various components:
    • Racial purity. His ancestors were all white.
    • Class purity. His ancestors were all from respectable classes.
    • Moral purity. None of his ancestors, especially the women, had spent time at the House of Corrections.
  51. Acclimated. The Creole is recommending that Frowenfeld adapt to the culture of New Orleans the way that he adapted to the climate. As a good American, Frowenfeld expects the world to adapt itself to him. It was one of the main objections of the locals to the Americans arriving after the Louisiana Purchase.
  52. Negrhophiles. 'Negrophiles' was a derogatory term used for whites who supported civil rights for black people. Cable was one of these and eventually had to leave the South because of it.
  53. À Louer. For rent.
  54. Porte cochère. Carriage gateway.
  55. En queue. In a pony tail.
  56. Rentier. Someone who lives off his investments rather than actually working for a living.
  57. Unconventional. Respectable ladies did not usually go out unaccompanied.
  58. Cession. Their term for the Louisiana Purchase. The formal handover had taken place earlier this week, December 20, 1803, at the Cabildo.
  59. Carondelet. Francisco Luis Hector, barón de Carondelet (1748 - 1807), Spanish governor of Louisiana 1791 - 1797.
  60. Governor Claiborne. William Charles Cole Claiborne (1772 - 1817). Governor of the Louisiana territory 1804-1812. First governor of the state of Louisiana 1812-1816.
  61. Venez ici. Come here.
  62. 'the air of this region is deadly to the Muses.' Source unknown. He is arguing that the atmosphere of Louisiana stifles art.
  63. Picayune. Half of a real, a Spanish coin. The real could be divided into 8 pieces, which were called "pieces of eight."
  64. Arpent. French unit of measuring land before the metric system. 1 arpent = 0.84628 acres.
  65. La belle langue. The beautiful language — French.
  66. Basilic. Basil.
  67. Quoi. What.
  68. Vien ici. Come here.
  69. Pistareen. Probably a corruption of the Spanish term "peseta." Spanish coin worth two reals.
  70. Tisane. Tea. Here he means a Voodoo tea to bring good fortune.
  71. porte-monnaie. Wallet.
  72. Bon soi,' Miché. Bonsoir — Good evening, Monsieur.
  73. Mo vien c'erc'er la bourse de Madame. "I'm here for Madame's purse."
  74. Oui, c'est ça. Yes, that's it.
  75. Mo pas compren'. I don't understand.
  76. Jaloff. The Jolof Empire controlled parts of Senegal, where many of the slaves in south Louisiana came from.
  77. Timonize. To become like Timon of Athens, a cranky loner who hates everybody.
  78. Jalousie. A window with wooden-slat louvered blinds.
  79. Polisson. Varmint.
  80. "Ah! maman! Ah! ma chère mère!" Ah, Mama! Ah, my dear Mama!
  81. Par example! For example.
  82. Papa Lébat. Father Jean Baptiste Lebat, a New Orleans priest who tried to stop voodoo. After his death, he was made a voodoo loa. As a man, he had tried to close doors; after his death, he became a diety of opening and closing doors, or in this case purses. Palmyre is a voodoo priestess.
  83. Helenium autumnale. Sneezeweed, a term Cable finds indelicate.
  84. Sirop naturelle. Natural syrup. Sugar cane syrup that had not been processed.
  85. Place d'Armes. Jackson Square.
  86. Sirop naturelle. Natural syrup. Sugar cane syrup that had not been processed.
  87. Place d'Armes. Jackson Square.
  88. Sieste. French translation of Siesta. An after-lunch nap during the hottest part of the day. Probably adopted locally during the period when the Spanish ruled Louisiana.
  89. Marchande des gâteaux. Cake merchant — she carried her calas and cakes around the city selling them.
  90. Carondelet. Francisco Luis Hector, Barón de Carondelet (1748-1807). Governor of the Spanish colony of Louisiana and West Florida, 1791-1797.
  91. Casa Calvo. Sebastián Calvo de la Puerta Y O'Farrill, Marqués de Casa Calvo (1751-1820). Governor of Spanish Louisiana 1799-1801. Returned to Louisiana during and after the Louisiana Purchase to attend to Spanish interests. Finally expelled in 1806.
  92. Daniel Clark. Dan iel Clark (1766-1813). Delegate from the Territory of Orleans (now the state of Louisiana) to the House of Representatives (1806-1809). Involved with land speculation and banking.
  93. Laussat. Pierre Clément de Laussat (1756-1835), last French Governor of Louisiana.
  94. Wilkerson. James Wilkerson, (1757-1825), first Governor of the Louisiana Territory (1805), served in the Revolutionary War and the War of 1812. Secretly worked as an agent for Spain.
  95. Miro. Esteban Rodriguez Miró y Sabater (1744 - 1795), Governor of Spanish Louisiana 1781-1791. Popular because he helped rebuild the city after the Great New Orleans Fire of 1788. Ft. Miró (later renamed Monroe) on the Ouachita River established during his governorship.
  96. Governor Gayoso. Manu el Luis Gayoso de Lemos Amorín y Magallanes (1747 - 1799), Spanish governor of Louisiana from 1797 until he died in 1799. He actually died of yellow fever.
  97. Edward Livingston. Edward Livingston (1764-1836) was the main leader in drafting the Louisiana Civil Code in 1825, based on the Napoleonic Code.
  98. Batture cases. A batture is the strip of land between the levee and the river. Livingston was involved with some cases where he and others made ownership claims on that land.
  99. Ex- Intendant Morales. Don Juan Ventura Morales served as the Intendendant of Louisiana under the Spanish rule.
  100. Knickerbockers. One original Dutch families to settle in New Amsterdam (New York). The name came to symbolize the old, original aristocracy before the English settlers, similar to the Creoles of Louisiana.
  101. Père et fils. Father and sons.
  102. Brothers Lafitte. Jean and Pierre Lafitte. Louisiana pirates who helped Andrew Jackson win the Battle or New Orleans in return for pardons.
  103. Marigny de Mandeville. Bernard Xavier Philippe de Marigny de Mandeville (1785-1868) was born in New Orleans and had both a title to French nobility and served as an American politician.
  104. Etienne de Boré. Boré (1741-1820) was originally from Illinois but was educated in France and joined the French military. Having served in Louisiana, he stayed after leaving the military. He developed a method for making granulated sugar, revolutionizing Louisiana agriculture and economics. Was also the first mayor of New Orleans.
  105. Madame Pontalba. Micaela Leonarda Antonia Almonester, Baroness de Pontalba (1795-1874) would have only been 9 years old at this time and a little young for marriage. When she did marry, her father-in-law was frustrated that she still had control of part of her wealth, so he kept her locked up several years and eventually shot her several times in the chest before killing himself. She somehow survived and became famous for her endurance.
  106. John McDonough. John McDonogh (1779-1850) was a businessman and philanthropist who left his fortune to provide public education to both white children and free black children.
  107. Julian Poydras. Julien de Lallande Poydras (1746-1824) was born in France and made his way to Louisiana. He founded the Female Orphan Asylum in New Orleans and left some of his fortune to provide poor girls with a dowry (money to take into a marriage).
  108. Yankee governor. William C. C. Claiborne (1772-1817) was the first American governor of Louisiana. The local Louisianans regarded him as a Yankee despite his being from Virginia.
  109. "Bon zou, Miché Honoré!". Bonjour (Good day), Monsieur Honoré! Cable portrays Clemence as using an informal form of French.
  110. Marchande. Merchant.
  111. "Comment to yé, Clemence?". "How are you, Clemence?"
  112. "Beau Miché, là,". "Good, Monsieur."
  113. "Mo pa'lé Anglé," "I speak (parlé) English."
  114. Mawse . Master.
  115. Calas . Calas were deep-fried rice cakes somewhat like beignets.
  116. Zinzer-cake. Ginger cake.
  117. 'Bé calas touts chauds'. "Lovely (belle) calas! Piping hot!"
  118. Fijinny. Virginia.
  119. "garde choual!" "Watch out!"
  120. "Animal, vous!" "You animal!"
  121. "Ya, ya! ye connais voudou bien!" "They're up in the voudou arts." [Author's note]
  122. Milatraisse. 'Mulatress' — feminine form of 'mulatto'; a person who had 50% white ancestry and 50% black ancestry.
  123. Tafia. Cheap rum made from sugar cane juice.
  124. Attendez. Hold on, wait.
  125. "Not in de groun'?" Only Jews and paupers are buried in the ground in New Orleans. [author's note]
  126. Trottoirs. Sidewalks.
  127. Ecoutez!. Listen!
  128. Abhaw. Abhor.
  129. Count O'Reilly. Alexander O'Reilly (Alejandro in Spanish) (1722-1794), originally from Ireland, served as the second Spanish governor of Louisiana. He put down the Louisiana Rebellion of 1768, a rebellion to throw off Spanish rule, and executed five of the ringleaders.
  130. Calinda dance. The Calinda is a combination of folk music and dance that arose in the Caribbean in the 1720s and spread from there to New Orleans.
  131. Distich. A couplet. A two-line stanza of poetry.
  132. "Miché Hon'ré, allé! h-allé!
    Trouvé to zamis parmi les Yankis,
    Dancé calinda, bou-joum! bou-joum!
    Dancé calinda, bou-joum! bou-joum!"

    "Mr. Honore, how are you!
    Find your friends among the Yankees
    Dance Calinda, good day, good day!
    Dance Calinda, good day, good day!"
  133. Prie-Dieu. A prie-dieu (literally 'pray to God') is a prayer desk with a small shelf and a kneeler.
  134. "Blinded alike . . ." From "The Eve of St. Agnes" by John Keats.
  135. Castile soap. Castile soap was soap from Spain made from olive oil. It was the first soap that was hard and white.
  136. Sticking-plaster. A sticking-plaster is a bandage used to cover a wound.
  137. Fête de grhandpère. Fête du grand père. Grandfather's birthday party.
  138. "Est il mort?" "Is he dead?"
  139. Zaguán. Vestibule (Spanish).
  140. "Dé 'tit zozos — ye té assis . . .

    Two little birds were sitting,
    Two little birds were sitting on the fence,
    Two little birds were twittering,
    What they were saying I do not know.

    A chicken hawk came along the road,
    Pounced on them and ate them up.
    No one hears the twittering anymore,
    The two little birds on the fence!
  141. "S'il vous plait, m'sieu'." If you please, Monsieur.
  142. "Godfrey's Cordial;". An opium-based medication. Parents sometimes used it to make their children go to sleep.
  143. "vous êtes astrologue — magicien — ". "You are an astrologer, a magician." To the locals of the era, the pharmacist with his potions looks a lot like the voodoo priest.
  144. 'P'tit Albert'. Petit Albert wrote an 18th century book of magic entitled Secrets Merveilleux de la Magie Naturelle et Cabalistique.
  145. Une ouangan, la nuit passé?. An ointment last night?
  146. Kerlerec. Louis Belcourt, Chevalier de Kerlerec (1704-1770) was the governor of the French colony of Louisiana from 1753 to 1763. He worked on defending the colony from the British during the French and Indian War (1754-1763).
  147. Rentier. Renter.
  148. Grigri. Talisman, voodoo amulet.
  149. i. e.. id est (Latin), that is.
  150. 'A la fin to vini!'. "I've reached the end!"
  151. Poudre d'amour. Love powder.

Source

Cable, George Washington. The Grandissimes: A Story of Creole Life. Illus. Albert Herter. New York: Charles Scribner's Sons, 1880, 1899. Internet Archive. Web. 30 May 2012. <http://archive.org/ details/ grandissimes00cabl>.

Anthology of Louisiana Literature
